A First Course in Ordinary  
Differential Equations
8 
Preface
Preface
In many cases continuous dynamical processes which occur in nature and in engineering
sciences can successfully be described by mathematical models that involve diï¬€erential
equations or systems of diï¬€erential equations. The solution of the initial-value problem or
the boundary-value problem described by a diï¬€erential equation with some limited infor-
mation about its solutions, can provide the full details and description of the dynamical
process. For example, Newtonâ€™s Second Law of Motion is in the form of a second-order
ordinary diï¬€erential equation that relates the second time-derivative of the position of a
mass and the total forces that act on the mass as it moves under these forces. Solving this
second-order diï¬€erential equation provides information on the velocity and the position
of the mass in terms of time. In fact, most physical theories are based on some funda-
mental diï¬€erential equation and are usually named after the scientist who ï¬rst derived
the equation: in quantum mechanics it is SchrÂ¨odingerâ€™s equation, in ï¬‚uid dynamics it is
the Navier-Stokes equation, in electrodynamics it is Maxwellâ€™s equations, in general rela-
tivity theory it is Einsteinâ€™s ï¬eld equations, in relativistic quantum mechanics it is Diracâ€™s
equations, etc. The mentioned equations are all very interesting diï¬€erential equations and
their solutions model many important natural processes. We should however point out
that the mentioned equations are mostly partial diï¬€erential equations or systems (meaning
that their dependent variables depend on several independent variables) and are moreover
often nonlinear and, therefore, are much more advanced than the diï¬€erential equations
that we study in the current set of lecture notes. In order to provide an introduction to
the general theory of diï¬€erential equations, we need to start with the simplest type of
equations, which are the linear ordinary diï¬€erential equations. Hereafter, referred
to simply as linear diï¬€erential equations.
The lecture notes presented here are intended for engineering and science students as
a ï¬rst course on diï¬€erential equations. It is assumed that the students have already read
a course on linear algebra, that included a discussion of general vector spaces, as well as a
course on integral calculus for functions that depend on one variable. However, no previous
knowledge of diï¬€erential equations is required to read and understand this material. Many
examples have been included in these notes and the proof of most statements are done in
full details. The aim of the notes is to provide the student with a thorough understanding
of the methods to obtain solutions of certain classes of diï¬€erential equations, rather than
the qualitative understanding of solutions and their existence. With the exception of some
nonlinear ï¬rst-order diï¬€erential equations, we concentrate on linear diï¬€erential equations
and the derivation of their solutions.

A First Course in Ordinary  
Differential Equations
9 
Preface
In Chapter 1 we provide the theoretical basis of the solution structure for linear dif-
ferential equations. This is an important part of the notes, as this chapter introduces the
concept of a linearly independent set of functions (or solutions) as well as the concept
of linear superposition. The Wronskian is introduced here to establish the linear inde-
pendence. In fact, the Wronskian plays a central role in the study of linear diï¬€erential
equations and it appears in many solution formulas throughout these lecture notes. The
solution methods described in Chapters 2 to 4 mostly involve AnsÂ¨atze for the solutions
of the diï¬€erential equations and, in some cases, we also need to introduce a change of
the variables in order to derive the solutions. In Appendix A we introduce an alternative
method to solve linear diï¬€erential equations based on ï¬rst-order linear operators and their
integral operators. This method is free from any Ansatz and can be viewed as an alter-
native to the solution methods proposed in Chapters 2 to 4. Appendix B sums up the
diï¬€erent techniques of integration, whereas Appendix C gives some references to books on
diï¬€erential equations. In Appendix D we give the full solutions of a selection of exercises
and in Appendix E we list the answers of all the exercises.
The four chapters included in this material can be taught in 15 lectures, which corre-
sponds to about 50% of a quarter-semester (8 weeks) course in Engineering Mathematics.
Norbert Euler
LuleËša, June 2015

Linearity and solutions
Chapter 1
Linear diï¬€erential equations and
linearly independent solutions
In this chapter we deï¬ne the diï¬€erent types of solutions that we will encounter in our
studies of diï¬€erential equations. We do not describe or propose in this chapter any methods
to solve diï¬€erential equations, as this is the main subject of the remaining chapters in these
notes. However, we prove here several fundamental results regarding the solution structure
of linear diï¬€erential equations and we also introduce the very important Wronskian of a
set of diï¬€erentiable functions, which makes it easy to establish the linear independence of
sets of solutions. This paves the way for several solution-methods for linear diï¬€erential
equation, studied in detail in chapters 2 to 4.
1.1
Solutions of diï¬€erential equations

Linearity and solutions
Graphically solutions of diï¬€erential equations may be depicted as curves in the XY -
plane on some interval D âŠ†R of the X-axis. For a ï¬rst-order equation, a general solution,
y(x) = Ï†(x; c1), contains one arbitrary constant (or parameter) c1. These solutions are
then one-parameter family of curves in the XY -plane. That is, for every ï¬xed choice of
c1 we obtain an explicit solution curve. This family of one-parameter solution curves are
also known as level curves. For second-order diï¬€erential equations a general solution,
y(x) = Ï†(x; c1, c2), contains two arbitrary constants, c1 and c2, so that this results in a
two-parameter family of curves in the XY -plane. The same holds for nth-order diï¬€erential
equations. Special solutions of a diï¬€erential equation are then the explicit solution curves
that result when choosing ï¬xed values for the constants of integration c1, c2, . . . , cn in
the given general solution. A singular solution of a diï¬€erential equation is a curve in the
XY -plane that does not belong to the family of curves as given by a general solution of
that equation. The singular solution curve may be an asymptote to the family of solution
curves given by a general solution.

a) Consider the set S = {1, ex} âˆˆC(R). Then it is clear that the equation

is satisï¬ed for all x âˆˆR if, for example, c1 = c2 = 1 and c3 = âˆ’1. Therefore we
conclude that S is a linearly dependent set in C(R).

Linearity and solutions
In order to give a rigorous proof of Proposition 1.1.5 we need the following theorem
on the existence and uniqueness of the solutions of (1.1.9) (the subject of existence and
uniqueness is outside the scope of these lecture notes and we will therefore not provide
the proof)
Proposition 1.1.6. (Existence and uniqueness theorem)
Consider the nth order homogeneous equation
pn(x)y(n) + pnâˆ’1(x)y(nâˆ’1) + Â· Â· Â· + p1(x)yâ€² + p0(x)y = 0,
where pj(x) are continuous and bounded on an interval D. For a given x0 âˆˆD and
given numbers b1, b2, . . . , bn, there exists a unique solution y(x) on D such that
y(x0) = b1,
yâ€²(x0) = b2,
. . . ,
y(nâˆ’1)(x0) = bn.
(1.1.39)
Note that Proposition (1.1.6) is also true for linear equations of the form
pn(x)y(n) + pnâˆ’1(x)y(nâˆ’1) + Â· Â· Â· + p1(x)yâ€² + p0(x)y = f(x),
where pj(x) and f(x) are continuous and bounded on D.

Proposition 1.2.2. Let T : y(x) â†’L y(x) with L deï¬ned by (1.2.3). Then the kernel

and

for all c âˆˆR

2.1
Introduction: the initial-value problem
A ï¬rst-order diï¬€erential equation is of the following general form:
F(x, y(x), yâ€²(x)) = 0.
(2.1.1)
In this section we introduce the so-called initial-value problem for ï¬rst-order equations and
then consider two types of ï¬rst-order diï¬€erential equations, namely the so-called separable
ï¬rst-order equations and the linear ï¬rst-order equation. We also include some cases of
ï¬rst-order diï¬€erential equations which can be written in the form of a separable diï¬€eren-
tial equation or a ï¬rst-order linear diï¬€erential equation by introducing a new dependent
variable.

The initial-value problem for ï¬rst-order diï¬€erential equations:
Let
y = Ï†(x; c)
(2.1.2)
be a general solution of (2.1.1) on some interval D âŠ†R, where Ï† contains an arbitrary
real constant c. The initial-value problem states the problem to ï¬nd the solution curve of
(2.1.1) which contains the point
y(x0) = b,
(2.1.3)
where x0 is a given point in the solution domain of the diï¬€erential equation and b is a given
real number. The relation (2.1.3) is known as the initial data and x0 is the initial value
for the solutions of the diï¬€erential equation. If the point x0 is in the solution domain of
the given general solution Ï†(x; c) and the general solution satisï¬es the initial data, then
the solution of the initial-value problem is obtained by solving the constant c from the
algebraic relation
Ï†(x0, c) = b.
(2.1.4)
The solution of this initial-value problem is then given by the general solution of (2.1.1)
where c is the explicit (unique) number that has been solved from the relation (2.1.4). If
the given initial data cannot be satisï¬ed by the given general solution y = Ï†(x; c1), that
is, if
Ï†(x0, c) Ì¸= b
for all c âˆˆR,
(2.1.5)
then the initial data may be in the domain of a singular solution for (2.1.1), say
y(x) = Ïˆ(x),
(2.1.6)
for which Ïˆ(x0) = b. In this case the solution to the initial-value problem is given by
the singular solution y(x) = Ïˆ(x). If neither the general solution, nor any of the singular
solutions for (2.1.1), satisfy the initial data y(x0) = b, then we say that this initial data
is inconsistent with the diï¬€erential equation, which means that the diï¬€erential equation
does not contain the point y(x0) = b for any of its solutions.
Several examples to illustrate the initial-value problem for ï¬rst-order diï¬€erential equa-
tions are given in the sections that follow.

Deï¬nition 2.3.1. The factor eG(x), where

Remark: A remark regarding Integrating Factors is in order: By the general theory of
ordinary diï¬€erential equations it is known that for any diï¬€erential equation that can be
integrated in closed form at least once (i.e. a diï¬€erential equation of order n for which the
order can be reduced by integration), there exists some integrating factor that brings the
equation to such an integrable form. To ï¬nd an integrating factor for a given diï¬€erential
equation is in general a diï¬ƒcult problem (depending strongly on the type of diï¬€erential
equation). In the case of ï¬rst-order linear diï¬€erential equations, the integrating factor,
exp[

g(x) dx], reduces the ï¬rst-order equation to a zero-order diï¬€erential equation, i.e.
we obtain a relation between y(x) and x and an arbitrary constant c from the integration;
hence we obtain a general solution of the ï¬rst-order diï¬€erential equation. It is a diï¬ƒcult
problem to ï¬nd integrating factors for nonlinear ï¬rst-order diï¬€erential equations, since the
problem is in general under-determined (in this sense ï¬rst-order diï¬€erential equations are
more complex than higher-order diï¬€erential equations, which is due to the geometry or
symmetry properties of these diï¬€erential equations).

3.1
Introduction: the initial- and boundary-value problem
A second-order diï¬€erential equation is of the general form
F(x, y(x), yâ€²(x), yâ€²â€²(x)) = 0
(3.1.1)
and its general solution is a function Ï† âˆˆC2(D) which contains two arbitrary constants,
c1 and c2, and satisï¬es the diï¬€erential equation. We write
y(x) = Ï†(x; c1, c2).
(3.1.2)
The initial value problem requires the following initial data in a point x0 in the solution
domain of the equation:
y(x0) = b0,
yâ€²(x0) = b1,
(3.1.3)
where b0 and b1 are given real numbers. This data is then used to ï¬x the constants c1 and
c2 in the general solution (if the initial data is within the domain of the general solution
and this data can be satisï¬ed by the general solution) by solving the (nonlinear) algebraic
system of equations
y(x0) = Ï†(x0; c1, c2) = b0,
yâ€²(x0) = dÏ†(x; c1, c2)
dx

x=x0
= b1.
(3.1.4)
If the diï¬€erential equation (3.1.1) is linear, then the algebraic system (3.1.4) is a system
of two linear algebraic equations in c1 and c2. This is clear since the constants c1 and c2
appear as weights in the linear combination of linearly independent solutions.
For the so-called boundary-value problem we require boundary data in two points,
x1 and x2, in the equationsâ€™ solution domain, namely
y(x1) = b1,
y(x2) = b2,

3.3
Particular solutions of nonhomogeneous linear second-
order diï¬€erential equations
We consider the linear second-order equation
yâ€²â€² + g(x)yâ€² + h(x)y = f(x)
(3.3.1)
where g(x), h(x) and f(x) are given continuous functions on some interval D âŠ†R. When
f(x) is not the zero function, then equation (3.3.1) is known as a nonhomogeneous
second-order linear diï¬€erential equation and a homogeneous second-order linear
diï¬€erential equation when f(x) is the zero function.
Deï¬nition 3.3.1. Any function yp(x) which satisï¬es the nonhomogeneous equation
(3.3.1) on an interval D and which does not contain two arbitrary constants, is known
as a particular solution for (3.3.1) on D.
The following proposition follows directly from the linear superposition principle:
Proposition 3.3.1.
a) A general solution of (3.3.1), i.e. equation
yâ€²â€² + g(x)yâ€² + h(x)y = f(x),
is of the form
y(x; c1, c2) = Ï†H(x; c1, c2) + yp(x),
(3.3.2)
where Ï†H is the general solution of the associated homogeneous equation yâ€²â€² +
g(x)yâ€²+h(x)y = 0 and yp is a particular solution of the nonhomogeneous equation
(3.3.1).
b) A particular solution yp(x) for the nonhomogeneous equation
yâ€²â€² + g(x)yâ€² + h(x)y = f1(x) + f2(x),
(3.3.3)
where g(x), h(x), f1(x) and f2(x) are given continuous functions on D, is given
by the sum
yp(x) = y1(x) + y2(x),
(3.3.4)
where y1(x) is a particular solution for yâ€²â€² + g(x)yâ€² + h(x)y = f1(x) and y2(x) is
a particular solution for yâ€²â€² + g(x)yâ€² + h(x)y = f2(x).
The proof is left as an exercise (see Exercises 3.3.2).

There exist several methods for ï¬nding particular solutions of (3.3.1) and we study
here two of those methods, namely the method of variation of parameters as well as the
method of undetermined coeï¬ƒcients. The former method can be applied for any continuous
functions g(x), h(x) and f(x), whereas the latter method is useful only if the coeï¬ƒcient
functions g(x) and h(x) are constants and the function f(x) is of special type.
The
advantage for the method of undetermined coeï¬ƒcients is that it does not involve any
integration as all steps are purely algebraic.

sition 3.3.2 below, is known as the method of variation of parameters.

Historical Note: (source: Wikipedia)
Gabriel Cramer (1704 â€“ 1752) was a Swiss mathematician, born in Geneva.
Cramer
showed promise in mathematics from an early age. At 18 he received his doctorate and
at 20 he was co-chair of mathematics.
Cramerâ€™s Rule for linear algebraic systems is
named after Gabriel Cramer, as he published the rule for an arbitrary number of unknowns
in 1750, although Colin Maclaurin also published special cases of the rule in 1748 (and
possibly knew of it as early as 1729). Cramer published his best-known work in his forties
in his treatise on algebraic curves (1750).

Historical Note: (source: Wikipedia)
Leonhard Euler (1707â€“1783) was a pioneering Swiss mathematician and physicist. He
made important discoveries in ï¬elds as diverse as inï¬nitesimal calculus and graph theory.
He also introduced much of the modern mathematical terminology and notation, partic-
ularly for mathematical analysis such as the notion of a mathematical function. He is
also renowned for his work in mechanics, ï¬‚uid dynamics, optics, and astronomy. Euler
is considered to be the pre-eminent mathematician of the 18th century and one of the
greatest mathematicians ever.
He is also one of the most proliï¬c mathematicians; his
collected works ï¬ll 80 volumes. He spent most of his adult life in St. Petersburg, Russia,
and in Berlin, Prussia. A statement attributed to Pierre-Simon Laplace expresses Eulerâ€™s
inï¬‚uence on mathematics: â€Read Euler, read Euler, he is the master of us all.â€

4.1
Introduction: the initial-value problem
In this chapter we consider the nth order (n â‰¥2) linear homogeneous ordinary dif-
ferential equation
pn(x)y(n) + pnâˆ’1(x)y(nâˆ’1) + Â· Â· Â· + p1(x)yâ€² + p0(x)y = 0
(4.1.1)

pn(x)y(n) + pnâˆ’1(x)y(nâˆ’1) + Â· Â· Â· + p1(x)yâ€² + p0(x)y = f(x)
(4.1.2)
Here pj(x) (j = 0, 1, 2, . . . , n) and f(x) are real-valued continuous functions given on some
common domain D âŠ†R, n â‰¥1 and pn(x) Ì¸= 0 for all x âˆˆD.
We know from Proposition 1.1.5 in Chapter 1, that the general solution of the ho-
mogeneous equation (4.1.1) is given by the linear combination of n linearly independent
solutions
S = {Ï†1(x), Ï†2(x), . . . , Ï†n(x)}
(4.1.3)
in Cn(D). That is, the general solution of (4.1.1) is

(4.1.4)

in Chapter 3, that the general solution of the second-order nonhomogeneous equation of
the form (4.1.2) (with n = 2) is given by the general solution of its homogeneous part plus
any particular solution yp(x) of the nonhomogeneous diï¬€erential equations. This is also
true for the n-th order nonhomogeneous equation (4.1.2), so that the general solution of
(4.1.2) is of the form

(4.1.5)

What are the diï¬ƒculties?
One should point out that the diï¬ƒculty of the task to ï¬nd the general solution of an n-th
order homogeneous equation with constant coeï¬ƒcients is due to the following questions:
1. How can we ï¬nd all the roots of the n-th degree characteristic polynomial equation
Pn(Î») := pnÎ»n + pnâˆ’1Î»nâˆ’1 + Â· Â· Â· + p1Î» + p0 = 0?
Although we know that n roots always exists, it is in general not possible to ï¬nd
them. However, there are some properties of n-degree polynomials that can be useful
to ï¬nd the roots (see Proposition 4.2.2 below).
2. How can we select n linearly independent solutions from the list of all solutions
obtained by the Ansatz
y(x) = eÎ»x,
for the roots Î»j of the corresponding characteristic equation?
This is in fact not a diï¬ƒcult problem and the answer is provided by Propositions
4.2.3.
3. How should we construct a suï¬ƒcient number of linearly independent solutions for the
case when the characteristic polynomial Pn(Î») admits roots with multiplicity k > 1,
i.e. when the same root appears k times (k âˆˆN)?
We provide a statement for this construction in Proposition 4.2.4.
We now address the above questions and provide general statements that will help
overcome the mentioned diï¬ƒculties.

The statement below follows directly from the linear superposition principle:

4.3.3
Particular solutions: the method of variation of parameters
We now describe the method of variation of parameters by generalizing Proposition
3.3.2 of Chapter 3, where we have studied this method for second-order equations. We
should recall that the method of variation of parameters is more general than the method
of undetermined coeï¬ƒcients, as it is applicable to equations which have continuous func-
tions as coeï¬ƒcients and it does not require a special form of the functions f(x) of the
nonhomogeneous part of the equation, neither does it require lots of diï¬€erent AnsÂ¨atze.
However, the derivation of particular solutions with this method does require the calcula-
tion of integrals, which can be diï¬ƒcult and tedious at times.

Appendix A: Integral operators: an alternative approach
Appendix A
Integral operators: an alternative
approach for solving linear
diï¬€erential equations
In this appendix we make use of linear operators to derive formulae for the general solution
of linear diï¬€erential equations of order n. This provides an alternative method, to those
proposed in the previous chapter, to ï¬nd the (general) solutions of these equations without
the need of any AnsÂ¨atze.. The key lies in the factorization of the linear diï¬€erential opera-
tors that determine the diï¬€erential equation in terms of ï¬rst-order diï¬€erential operators.
For linear equations with nonconstant coeï¬ƒcients, the condition for the factorization of
the operators is in the form of Riccati equations. This method also provides an alterna-
tive method to the method of variation of parameters and the method of undetermined
coeï¬ƒcients for the calculation of particular solutions of linear nonhomogeneous equations,
which we studied in Chapters 3 and 4.
A.1

responds to a general ï¬rst-order linear diï¬€erential operator. This integral operator is the
key to the integration of the linear equations.

main D âŠ†R and Cn(D) the subspace of C(D) consisting of all n-continuously diï¬€erentiable
functions on D.

A.2
Higher-order linear constant-coeï¬ƒcient diï¬€erential equa-
tions
In order to apply the method of linear operators and their corresponding integral operators
to solve higher-order linear diï¬€erential equations, we need to factorize the higher-order
linear operators that determine the diï¬€erential equations in terms of ï¬rst-order operators.
This is in principle always possible, but in practise there are some obstacles.

terms of ï¬rst-order diï¬€erential operators in the same manner as the characteristic equation
